<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Boeing 787 Dreamliner</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

<p>The <b>Boeing 787 Dreamliner</b> is an American <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-haul" title="Long-haul">long-haul</a>, mid-size <a href="/wiki/Wide-body_aircraft" title="Wide-body aircraft">widebody</a>, twin-engine <a href="/wiki/Jet_airliner" title="Jet airliner">jet airliner</a> made by <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Commercial_Airplanes" title="Boeing Commercial Airplanes">Boeing Commercial Airplanes</a>. Its variants seat 242 to 335 passengers in typical three-class seating configurations. It is Boeing's most <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fuel-efficient" title="Fuel-efficient">fuel-efficient</a> airliner and is a pioneering airliner with the use of <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a> as the primary material in the construction of its <a href="/wiki/Airframe" title="Airframe">airframe</a>. The 787 was designed to be 20% more fuel efficient than the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767</a>, which it was intended to replace. The 787 Dreamliner's distinguishing features include mostly electrical flight systems, <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Raked_wingtip" title="Wingtip device">raked wingtips</a>, and noise-reducing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevron_(aeronautics)" title="Chevron (aeronautics)">chevrons</a> on its engine <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a>. It shares a common <a href="/wiki/Type_rating" title="Type rating">type rating</a> with the larger <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a> to allow qualified <a href="/wiki/Pilot_(aeronautics)" title="Pilot (aeronautics)">pilots</a> to operate both models.</p>
<p>The aircraft's initial designation was the <b>7E7</b>, prior to its renaming in January 2005. The first 787 was unveiled in a roll-out ceremony on July 8, 2007 at Boeing's Everett factory. Development and production of the 787 has involved a large-scale collaboration with numerous suppliers worldwide. Final assembly takes place at the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Everett_Factory" title="Boeing Everett Factory">Boeing Everett Factory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Everett,_Washington" title="Everett, Washington">Everett, Washington</a>, and at the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_South_Carolina" title="Boeing South Carolina">Boeing South Carolina</a> factory in <a href="/wiki/North_Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="North Charleston, South Carolina">North Charleston, South Carolina</a>. Originally planned to enter service in May 2008, the project experienced multiple delays. The airliner's <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">maiden flight</a> took place on December 15, 2009, and completed flight testing in mid-2011. Boeing has reportedly spent $32 billion on the 787 program.</p>
<p>Final US <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> (FAA) and <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">European Aviation Safety Agency</a> (EASA) <a href="/wiki/Type_certificate" title="Type certificate">type certification</a> was received in August 2011 and the first 787-8 was delivered in September 2011. It entered commercial service on October 26, 2011 with launch customer <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a>. The stretched 787-9 variant, which is 20 feet (6.1 m) longer and can fly 450 nautical miles (830 km) farther than the -8, first flew in September 2013. Deliveries of the 787-9 began in July 2014; it entered commercial service on August 7, 2014 with All Nippon Airways, with 787-9 launch customer <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a> following two days later. As of December 2016, the 787 had orders for 1,200 aircraft from 64 customers, with All Nippon Airways having the largest number on order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_O_D_summ-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The aircraft has suffered from several <a href="#Operational_problems">in-service problems</a>, including fires on board related to its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_batteries" title="Lithium-ion batteries">lithium-ion batteries</a>. These systems were reviewed by both the FAA and the <a href="/wiki/Japan_Civil_Aviation_Bureau" title="Japan Civil Aviation Bureau">Japan Civil Aviation Bureau</a>. The FAA issued a directive in January 2013 that grounded all 787s in the US and other civil aviation authorities followed suit. After Boeing completed tests on a revised battery design, the FAA approved the revised design and lifted the grounding in April 2013; the 787 returned to passenger service later that month.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span></h3>
<p>During the late 1990s, Boeing considered replacement aircraft programs as sales of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">767</a> and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400" title="Boeing 747-400">747-400</a> slowed. Two new aircraft were proposed, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747#747X_and_747X_Stretch" title="Boeing 747">747X</a>, which would have lengthened the 747-400 and improved efficiency, and the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Sonic_Cruiser" title="Boeing Sonic Cruiser">Sonic Cruiser</a>, which would have achieved 15% higher speeds (approximately <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a> 0.98) while burning fuel at the same rate as the 767.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-need-speed_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-need-speed-4">[4]</a></sup> Market interest for the 747X was tepid, however several major American airlines, including <a href="/wiki/Continental_Airlines" title="Continental Airlines">Continental Airlines</a>, showed initial enthusiasm for the Sonic Cruiser, although concerns about the operating cost were also expressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_Sonic_Cruiser_20010528_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes_Sonic_Cruiser_20010528-5">[5]</a></sup> The global airline market was disrupted by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a> and increased petroleum prices, making airlines more interested in efficiency than speed. The worst-affected airlines, those in the United States, had been considered the most likely customers of the Sonic Cruiser; thus the Sonic Cruiser was officially cancelled on December 20, 2002. On January 29, 2003 Boeing announced an alternative product, the 7E7, using Sonic Cruiser technology in a more conventional configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The emphasis on a smaller midsize twinjet rather than a large 747-size aircraft represented a shift from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spoke-hub_distribution_paradigm" title="Spoke-hub distribution paradigm">hub-and-spoke theory</a> towards the <a href="/wiki/Point-to-point_transit" title="Point-to-point transit">point-to-point theory</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aerlines_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aerlines-8">[8]</a></sup> in response to analysis of <a href="/wiki/Focus_group" title="Focus group">focus groups</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BabejPollak_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-BabejPollak-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_logo.svg"><img alt="The Dreamliner logo" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="14" data-file-width="53" height="58" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dreamliner_logo.svg/220px-Dreamliner_logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dreamliner_logo.svg/330px-Dreamliner_logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Dreamliner_logo.svg/440px-Dreamliner_logo.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_logo.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Dreamliner</i> was announced in July 2003. This logo is painted on many 787s.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The replacement for the Sonic Cruiser project was named "7E7"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> (with a development code name of "Y2"). Technology from the Sonic Cruiser and 7E7 was to be used as part of Boeing's project to replace its entire airliner product line, an endeavor called the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Yellowstone_Project" title="Boeing Yellowstone Project">Yellowstone Project</a> (of which the 7E7 became the first stage).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boe_787_power_on_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boe_787_power_on-11">[11]</a></sup> Early concept images of the 7E7 included rakish <a href="/wiki/Cockpit" title="Cockpit">cockpit</a> windows, a dropped nose and a distinctive "shark-fin" <a href="/wiki/Vertical_stabilizer" title="Vertical stabilizer">tail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The "E" was said to stand for various things, such as "efficiency" or "environmentally friendly"; however, in the end, Boeing said that it merely stood for "Eight".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> In July 2003, a public naming competition was held for the 7E7, for which out of 500,000 votes cast online the winning title was <i>Dreamliner</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Other names included <i>eLiner</i>, <i>Global Cruiser</i>, and <i>Stratoclimber</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200940_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200940-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg"><img alt="B787 in launch customer All Nippon Airways' blue and white livery. In the background are two assembly halls, with huge doors facing left. Vehicles are parked in front of the halls." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg/220px-All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg/330px-All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg/440px-All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:All_Nippon_Airways_Boeing_787_Dreamliner_two.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
All Nippon Airways launched the 787 program with an order for 50 aircraft in 2004.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On April 26, 2004, Japanese airline <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> (ANA) became the launch customer for the 787, announcing a firm order for 50 aircraft with deliveries to begin in late 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> The ANA order was initially specified as 30 787-3, 290–330 seat, one-class domestic aircraft, and 20 787-8, long-haul, 210–250 seat, two-class aircraft for regional international routes such as Tokyo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narita_Airport" title="Narita Airport">Narita</a>–Beijing, and could perform routes to cities not previously served, such as <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The 787-3 and 787-8 were to be the initial variants, with the 787-9 entering service in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-start_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-start-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 787 was designed to be the first production airliner with the fuselage comprising one-piece <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite</a> barrel sections instead of the multiple aluminum sheets and some 50,000 fasteners used on existing aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rdmag_200707_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-rdmag_200707-20">[20]</a></sup> Boeing selected two new engines to power the 787, the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_1000" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 1000">Rolls-Royce Trent 1000</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GEnx" title="General Electric GEnx">General Electric GEnx</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> Boeing stated the 787 would be approximately 20 percent more fuel-efficient than the 767,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AviationWeek_2009_0109_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-AviationWeek_2009_0109-21">[21]</a></sup> with approximately 40 percent of the efficiency gain from the engines,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> plus gains from aerodynamic improvements,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200948_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200948-23">[23]</a></sup> increased use of lighter-weight composite materials, and advanced systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-start_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-start-18">[18]</a></sup> The airframe underwent extensive structural testing during its design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The 787-8 and −9 were intended to have a certified 330 minute <a href="/wiki/ETOPS" title="ETOPS">ETOPS</a> capability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the design phase, the 787 underwent extensive <a href="/wiki/Wind_tunnel" title="Wind tunnel">wind tunnel</a> testing at Boeing's Transonic Wind Tunnel, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/QinetiQ" title="QinetiQ">QinetiQ</a>'s five-meter wind tunnel at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airfield" title="Farnborough Airfield">Farnborough</a>, United Kingdom, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NASA_Ames_Research_Center" title="NASA Ames Research Center">NASA Ames Research Center</a>'s wind tunnel, as well as at the French aerodynamics research agency, <a href="/wiki/ONERA" title="ONERA">ONERA</a>. The final styling was more conservative than earlier proposals, with the fin, nose, and cockpit windows changed to a more conventional form. By 2005, customer-announced orders and commitments for the 787 reached 237 aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Boeing initially priced the 787-8 variant at US$120 million, a low figure that surprised the industry. In 2007, the list price was US$146–151.5 million for the 787-3, US$157–167 million for the 787-8 and US$189–200 million for the 787-9.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Manufacturing_and_suppliers">Manufacturing and suppliers</span></h3>
<p>On December 16, 2003, Boeing announced that the 787 would be assembled in its <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Everett_Factory" title="Boeing Everett Factory">factory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Everett,_Washington" title="Everett, Washington">Everett, Washington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> Instead of conventionally building the aircraft from the ground up, final assembly employed 800 to 1,200 people to join completed subassemblies and to integrate systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bca_20061206_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-bca_20061206-29">[29]</a></sup> Boeing assigned global subcontractors to do more assembly work, delivering completed subassemblies to Boeing for final assembly. This approach was intended to result in a leaner, simpler assembly line and lower inventory,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> with pre-installed systems reducing final assembly time by three-quarters to three days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> Subcontractors had early difficulties procuring needed parts and finishing subassemblies on schedule, leaving remaining assembly work for Boeing to complete as "traveled work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> In 2010, Boeing considered in-house construction of the 787-9 tail; the tail of the 787-8 is made by Alenia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The 787 was unprofitable for some subcontractors; Alenia's parent company, Finmeccanicam had a total loss of €750 million on the project by 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1152" data-file-width="1728" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg/220px-Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg/330px-Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg/440px-Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Section_41_final_assembly.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assembly of "Section 41", the nose section of the Boeing 787</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Subcontracted assemblies included wing manufacture (<a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a>, Japan, central wing box)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Tailplane" title="Tailplane">horizontal stabilizers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Alenia_Aeronautica" title="Alenia Aeronautica">Alenia Aeronautica</a>, Italy; <a href="/wiki/Korea_Aerospace_Industries" title="Korea Aerospace Industries">Korea Aerospace Industries</a>, South Korea);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> fuselage sections (Global Aeronautica, Italy; Boeing, <a href="/wiki/North_Charleston,_South_Carolina" title="North Charleston, South Carolina">North Charleston</a>, US; <a href="/wiki/Kawasaki_Heavy_Industries" title="Kawasaki Heavy Industries">Kawasaki Heavy Industries</a>, Japan; <a href="/wiki/Spirit_AeroSystems" title="Spirit AeroSystems">Spirit AeroSystems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita</a>, US; <a href="/wiki/Korean_Air" title="Korean Air">Korean Air</a>, South Korea);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seatimes_20050911_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-seatimes_20050911-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> passenger doors (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latecoere_group" title="Latecoere group">Latécoère</a>, France); cargo doors, access doors, and crew escape door (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saab_AB" title="Saab AB">Saab AB</a>, Sweden); software development (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HCL_Enterprise" title="HCL Enterprise">HCL Enterprise</a> India);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> floor beams (<a href="/wiki/Tata_Group" title="Tata Group">TAL Manufacturing Solutions Limited</a>, India);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> wiring (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Labinal" title="Labinal">Labinal</a>, France);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_team_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_team-45">[45]</a></sup> wing-tips, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flap_(aircraft)" title="Flap (aircraft)">flap</a> support fairings, wheel well bulkhead, and <a href="/wiki/Longeron" title="Longeron">longerons</a> (Korean Air, South Korea);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KAL_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-KAL-46">[46]</a></sup> landing gear (<a href="/wiki/Messier-Bugatti-Dowty" title="Messier-Bugatti-Dowty">Messier-Bugatti-Dowty</a>, UK/France);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> and power distribution and management systems, air conditioning packs (<a href="/wiki/Hamilton_Sundstrand" title="Hamilton Sundstrand">Hamilton Sundstrand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, US).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_team_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_team-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-air_conditioning_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-air_conditioning-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>To speed up deliveries, Boeing modified four used <a href="/wiki/Boeing_747-400" title="Boeing 747-400">747-400s</a> into <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Dreamlifter" title="Boeing Dreamlifter">747 Dreamlifters</a> to transport 787 wings, fuselage sections, and other smaller parts. Japanese industrial participation was key on the project. Japanese companies co-designed and built 35% of the aircraft; the first time that outside firms played a key design role on Boeing airliner wings. The Japanese government supported development with an estimated US$2 billion in loans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_shares_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_shares-50">[50]</a></sup> On April 26, 2006, Japanese manufacturer <a href="/wiki/Toray_Industries" title="Toray Industries">Toray Industries</a> and Boeing signed a production agreement involving US$6 billion worth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon_fiber" title="Carbon fiber">carbon fiber</a>, extending a 2004 contract.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-4"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> In May 2007, final assembly on the first 787 began at Everett.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boeing worked to trim excess weight since assembly of the first airframe began; in late 2006, the first six 787s were stated to be overweight, with the first aircraft being 5,000 lb (2,300 kg) heavier than specified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FG_2006_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FG_2006-52">[52]</a></sup> The seventh and subsequent aircraft would be the first optimized 787-8s expected to meet all goals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KOMOTV_News_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-KOMOTV_News-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Financial_Times_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Financial_Times-54">[54]</a></sup> Accordingly, some parts were redesigned to include more use of <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spi_20061207_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-spi_20061207-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Early built 787s were overweight and some carriers decided to take later aircraft; in early 2015, Boeing was trying to sell 10 such aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In July 2015, Reuters reported that Boeing was considering reducing the use of titanium to reduce construction costs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg"><img alt="The Everett Factory Hall's huge door opens as the first 787 is rolled out. Surrounding the aircraft are guests and the public." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="1022" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg/220px-Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg/330px-Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg/440px-Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Roll-out.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first public appearance of the 787 on July 8, 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Boeing intended for a first flight by the end of August 2007 and premiered the first 787 at a rollout ceremony on July 8, 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> The 787 had 677 orders at this time, which is more orders from launch to roll-out than any previous wide-body airliner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-677_orders_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-677_orders-60">[60]</a></sup> The major systems were not installed at the time; many parts were attached with temporary non-aerospace <a href="/wiki/Fastener" title="Fastener">fasteners</a> requiring replacement with flight fasteners later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Flight_Global_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Flight_Global-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>In September 2007, Boeing announced a three-month delay, blaming a shortage of fasteners as well as incomplete software.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomb_delay1_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bloomb_delay1-62">[62]</a></sup> On October 10, 2007, a second three-month delay to the first flight and a six-month delay to first deliveries was announced due to supply chain problems, a lack of documentation from overseas suppliers, and flight guidance software delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ny_times_20071010_delivery_delay_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-ny_times_20071010_delivery_delay-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Less than a week later, Mike Bair, the 787 program manager was replaced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> On January 16, 2008, Boeing announced a third three-month delay to the first flight of the 787, citing insufficient progress on "traveled work".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> On March 28, 2008, in an effort to gain more control over the supply chain, Boeing announced plans to buy Vought Aircraft Industries' interest in Global Aeronautica; a later agreement was also made to buy Vought's factory in North Charleston.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 9, 2008, a fourth delay was announced, shifting the <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">maiden flight</a> to the fourth quarter of 2008, and delaying initial deliveries by around 15 months to the third quarter of 2009. The 787-9 variant was postponed to 2012 and the 787-3 variant was to follow at a later date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_variants_delayed_to_at_least_2012_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_variants_delayed_to_at_least_2012-68">[68]</a></sup> On November 4, 2008, a fifth delay was announced due to incorrect fastener installation and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boeing_Machinists_Strike_of_2008" title="Boeing Machinists Strike of 2008">Boeing machinists strike</a>, stating that the first test flight would not occur in the fourth quarter of 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> After assessing the program schedule with suppliers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> in December 2008, Boeing stated that the first flight was delayed until the second quarter of 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Airlines, such as <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Air_India" title="Air India">Air India</a>, stated their intentions to seek <a href="/wiki/Financial_compensation" title="Financial compensation">compensation</a> from Boeing for the delays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-flight_ground_testing">Pre-flight ground testing</span></h3>
<p>As Boeing worked with its suppliers towards production, the design proceeded through a series of test goals. On August 23, 2007, a crash test involving a vertical drop of a partial composite fuselage section from about 15 ft (4.6 m) onto a 1 in (25 mm)-thick steel plate occurred in <a href="/wiki/Mesa,_Arizona" title="Mesa, Arizona">Mesa, Arizona</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crash_Test_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Crash_Test-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_News_2008_0829_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Design_News_2008_0829-76">[76]</a></sup> the results matched predictions, allowing modeling of various crash scenarios using computational analysis instead of further physical tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forbes_2007_0906_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes_2007_0906-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_News_2007_0906_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Design_News_2007_0906-78">[78]</a></sup> While critics had expressed concerns that a composite fuselage could shatter and burn with toxic fumes during crash landings, test data indicated no greater toxicity than conventional metal airframes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_09_18_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes_09_18-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drops_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-drops-80">[80]</a></sup> The crash test was the third in a series of demonstrations conducted to match FAA requirements, including additional certification criteria due to the wide-scale use of composite materials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_News_2008_0829_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-Design_News_2008_0829-76">[76]</a></sup> The 787 meets the FAA's requirement that passengers have at least as good a chance of surviving a crash landing as they would with current metal airliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2137" data-file-width="3450" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg/220px-Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg/330px-Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg/440px-Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_first_flight_taxi_turn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The prototype Boeing 787 underwent taxi tests at <a href="/wiki/Paine_Field" title="Paine Field">Paine Field</a> in November and December 2009.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On August 7, 2007, on-time certification of the <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_1000" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 1000">Rolls-Royce Trent 1000</a> engine by European and US regulators was received.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> The alternative GE GEnx-1B engine achieved certification on March 31, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> On June 20, 2008, the first aircraft was powered up, for testing the electrical supply and distribution systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated2_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated2-84">[84]</a></sup> A non-flightworthy static test airframe was built; on September 27, 2008, the fuselage was successfully tested at 14.9 <a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a> (102.7 kPa) differential, which is 150 percent of the maximum pressure expected in commercial service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> In December 2008, the 787's maintenance program was passed by the FAA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 3, 2009, the first test 787 was moved to the flight line following extensive factory-testing, including landing gear swings, systems integration verification, and a total run-through of the first flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flightline_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-flightline-87">[87]</a></sup> On May 4, 2009, a press report indicated a 10–15% range reduction, about 6,900 nmi (12,800 km) instead of the originally promised 7,700 to 8,200 nmi (14,800–15,700 km), for early aircraft that were about 8% overweight. Substantial redesign work was expected to correct this, which would complicate increases in production rates;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ATW_May_4.2C_09_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ATW_May_4.2C_09-88">[88]</a></sup> Boeing stated the early 787-8s would have a range of almost 8,000 nmi (15,000 km).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_confirms_weight_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_confirms_weight-89">[89]</a></sup> As a result, some airlines reportedly delayed deliveries of 787s in order to take later planes that may be closer to the original estimates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Boeing expected to have the weight issues addressed by the 21st production model.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shanghai_casts_doubt_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shanghai_casts_doubt-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>On June 15, 2009, during the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Air_Show" title="Paris Air Show">Paris Air Show</a>, Boeing said that the 787 would make its first flight within two weeks. However, on June 23, 2009, the first flight was postponed due to structural reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Boeing provided an updated 787 schedule on August 27, 2009, with the first flight planned to occur by the end of 2009 and deliveries to begin at the end of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boeing.mediaroom.com_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-boeing.mediaroom.com-94">[94]</a></sup> The company expected to write off US$2.5 billion because it considered the first three Dreamliners built unsellable and suitable only for flight tests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> On October 28, 2009, Boeing selected Charleston, SC as the site for a second 787 production line, after soliciting bids from multiple states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sc_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-sc-96">[96]</a></sup> On December 12, 2009, the first 787 completed high speed taxi tests, the last major step before flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FG_final_gaunlet_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FG_final_gaunlet-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_test_program">Flight test program</span></h3>
<p>On December 15, 2009, Boeing conducted the Dreamliner's <a href="/wiki/Maiden_flight" title="Maiden flight">maiden flight</a> with the first 787-8, originating from <a href="/wiki/Paine_Field" title="Paine Field">Snohomish County Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Everett,_Washington" title="Everett, Washington">Everett</a>, Washington, at 10:27 am PST,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FG_takeoff_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FG_takeoff-99">[99]</a></sup> and landing at <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Field" title="Boeing Field">Boeing Field</a> in <a href="/wiki/King_County,_Washington" title="King County, Washington">King County</a>, Washington, at 1:35 pm PST.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Originally scheduled for four hours, the test flight was shortened to three hours due to bad weather.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Boeing's schedule called for a 9-month flight test campaign (later revised to 8.5 months).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2038" data-file-width="3833" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg/220px-Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg/330px-Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg/440px-Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_first_flight.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first 787 built taking off on its maiden flight.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The flight test program comprised six aircraft, ZA001 through ZA006, four with <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_1000" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 1000">Rolls-Royce Trent 1000</a> engines and two with GE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GEnx" title="GEnx">GEnx</a>-1B64 engines. The second 787, ZA002 in All Nippon Airways <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_livery" title="Aircraft livery">livery</a>, flew to <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Field" title="Boeing Field">Boeing Field</a> on December 22, 2009, to join the flight test program;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> the third 787, ZA004 made its first flight on February 24, 2010, followed by ZA003 on March 14, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787flighttest_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-787flighttest-106">[106]</a></sup> On March 24, 2010, flutter and ground effects testing was completed, clearing the aircraft to fly its entire <a href="/wiki/Flight_envelope" title="Flight envelope">flight envelope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> On March 28, 2010, the 787 completed the ultimate wing load test, which requires that the wings of a fully assembled aircraft be loaded to 150% of design limit load and held for 3 seconds. The wings were flexed approximately 25 ft (7.6 m) upward during the test.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Unlike past aircraft, the wings were not tested to failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> On April 7, data showed the test had been a success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 23, 2010, the newest 787, ZA003, arrived at the <a href="/wiki/McKinley_Climatic_Laboratory" title="McKinley Climatic Laboratory">McKinley Climatic Laboratory</a> hangar at <a href="/wiki/Eglin_Air_Force_Base" title="Eglin Air Force Base">Eglin Air Force Base</a>, Florida, for extreme weather testing in temperatures ranging from 115 to −45 °F (46 to −43 °C), including takeoff preparations at both temperature extremes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> ZA005, the fifth 787 and the first with GEnx engines, began ground engine tests in May 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and made its first flight on June 16, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> In June 2010, gaps were discovered in the horizontal stabilizers of test aircraft due to improperly installed shims; all aircraft were inspected and repaired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> That same month, a 787 experienced its first in-flight lightning strike; inspections found no damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.wired.com_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.wired.com-116">[116]</a></sup> As composites can have as little as 1/1,000th the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrical_conductivity" title="Electrical conductivity">electrical conductivity</a> of aluminum, conductive material is added to ameliorate potential risks and to meet FAA requirements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_09_18_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes_09_18-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> The FAA also planned requirement changes to help the 787 show compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_2009_02_08_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes_2009_02_08-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="725" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg/220px-B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg/330px-B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg/440px-B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:B787_flyby_Farnborough.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first 787 to visit Europe, ZA003 at the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787 made its first appearance at an international air show at the <a href="/wiki/Farnborough_Airshow" title="Farnborough Airshow">Farnborough Airshow</a>, United Kingdom, on July 18, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>On August 2, 2010, a Trent 1000 engine suffered a blowout at Rolls-Royce's test facility during ground testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloomTrent_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-bloomTrent-121">[121]</a></sup> The failure due to the timeline for installing Trent 1000 engines being reevaluated; on August 27, 2010, Boeing stated that the first delivery to launch customer ANA would be delayed until early 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc_delay_2011_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_delay_2011-122">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fgTrent_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-fgTrent-123">[123]</a></sup> That same month, Boeing .<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> In September 2010, it was reported that a further two 787s might join the test fleet for a total of eight flight test aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> On September 10, 2010, a partial engine surge occurred in a Trent engine on ZA001 at Roswell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-awSurge_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-awSurge-126">[126]</a></sup> On October 4, 2010, the sixth 787, ZA006 joined the test program with its first flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 9, 2010, Boeing 787, ZA002 made an emergency landing at <a href="/wiki/Laredo_International_Airport" title="Laredo International Airport">Laredo International Airport</a>, Texas, after smoke and flames were detected in the main cabin during a test flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787fireovertexas_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-787fireovertexas-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The electrical fire caused some systems to fail before landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Following this incident, Boeing suspended flight testing on November 10, 2010, ground testing continued.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> After investigation, the in-flight fire was primarily attributed to <a href="/wiki/Foreign_object_damage" title="Foreign object damage">foreign object debris</a> (FOD) that was present in the electrical bay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> After electrical system and software changes, the 787 resumed flight testing on December 23, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 5, 2010, it was reported that some 787 deliveries would be delayed to address problems found during flight testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> In January 2011, the first 787 delivery was rescheduled to the third quarter of 2011 due to software and electrical updates following the in-flight fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> By February 24, 2011, the 787 had completed 80% of the test conditions for Rolls-Royce Trent 1000 engine and 60% of the conditions for the General Electric GEnx-1B engine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> In July 2011, ANA performed a week of operations testing using a 787 in Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> The test aircraft had flown 4,828 hours in 1,707 flights combined by August 15, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787flighttest_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-787flighttest-106">[106]</a></sup> During testing, the 787 visited 14 countries in Asia, Europe, North America, and South America to test in extreme climates and conditions and for route testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> On August 13, 2011, certification testing of the Rolls-Royce powered 787-8 finished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> The FAA and <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">European Aviation Safety Agency</a> certified the 787 on August 26, 2011, at a ceremony in Everett, Washington.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faa_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-faa-145">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>The newer stretched version, 787-9, had flown 141 hours as of November 8, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_entry_and_early_operations">Service entry and early operations</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2141" data-file-width="3850" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg/220px-Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg/330px-Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg/440px-Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_Dreamliner_arrival_Airventure_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 787-8 received FAA and EASA certification on August 21, 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1819" data-file-width="2731" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_G-ZBJB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> 787-8 landing at <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Pearson_International_Airport" title="Toronto Pearson International Airport">Toronto Pearson International Airport</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Certification cleared the way for deliveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faa_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-faa-145">[145]</a></sup> In 2011, Boeing prepared to increase 787 production rates from two to ten aircraft per month at assembly lines in Everett and Charleston over two years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faa_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-faa-145">[145]</a></sup> Legal difficulties clouded production at Charleston; on April 20, 2011, the <a href="/wiki/National_Labor_Relations_Board" title="National Labor Relations Board">National Labor Relations Board</a> alleged that a second production line in South Carolina violated two sections of the National Labor Relations Act.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sc_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-sc-96">[96]</a></sup> In December 2011, the National Labor Relations Board dropped its lawsuit after the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_of_Machinists_and_Aerospace_Workers" title="International Association of Machinists and Aerospace Workers">Machinists union</a> withdrew its complaint as part of a new contract with Boeing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-147">[147]</a></sup> The first 787 assembled at South Carolina was rolled out on April 27, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-debutssc_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-debutssc-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first 787 was officially delivered to All Nippon Airways (ANA) on September 25, 2011, at the Boeing factory. A ceremony to mark the occasion was also held the next day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FG_Formal_Delivery_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-FG_Formal_Delivery-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boe_Contractual_Delivery_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boe_Contractual_Delivery-150">[150]</a></sup> On September 27, it flew to Tokyo <a href="/wiki/Haneda_Airport" title="Haneda Airport">Haneda Airport</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> The airline took delivery of the second 787 on October 13, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ST_2nd_787_delivered_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-ST_2nd_787_delivered-153">[153]</a></sup> On October 26, 2011, an ANA 787 flew the first commercial flight from Tokyo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narita_Airport" title="Narita Airport">Narita</a> to <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_International_Airport" title="Hong Kong International Airport">Hong Kong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_Dreamliner_1st_pax_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_Dreamliner_1st_pax-154">[154]</a></sup> The airliner was planned to enter service some three years prior. Tickets for the flight were sold in an online auction, the highest bidder had paid $34,000 for a seat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> An ANA 787 flew the first commercial long-haul flight on January 21, 2012 from Haneda to <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Airport" title="Frankfurt Airport">Frankfurt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>On December 6, 2011, test aircraft ZA006 (sixth 787), powered by General Electric GEnx engines, flew 10,710 nautical miles (19,830 km) non-stop from Boeing Field eastward to <a href="/wiki/Shahjalal_International_Airport" title="Shahjalal International Airport">Shahjalal International Airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dhaka" title="Dhaka">Dhaka</a>, Bangladesh, setting a new world distance record for aircraft in the 787's weight class, which is between 440,000 and 550,000 pounds (200,000 and 250,000 kg). This flight surpassed the previous record of 9,127 nautical miles (16,903 km), set in 2002 by an <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a>. The Dreamliner then continued eastbound from Dhaka to return to Boeing Field, setting a world-circling speed record of 42 hours, 27 minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> In April 2012, an ANA 787 made a delivery flight from Boeing Field to <a href="/wiki/Haneda_Airport" title="Haneda Airport">Haneda Airport</a> partially using <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuel</a> from cooking oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> In 2011, Boeing conducted a promotion 787 world tour, visiting various cities in China, Africa, the Middle East, Europe, United States, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to ANA data, the 787 surpassed the promised 20% fuel burn reduction comparison to the 767, achieving a 21% fuel saving on the Tokyo-Frankfurt route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANA_efficiency_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANA_efficiency-161">[161]</a></sup> ANA surveyed 800 passengers who flew the 787 from Tokyo to Frankfurt: expectations were surpassed for 90% of passengers; features that met or exceeded expectations included air quality and cabin pressure (90% of passengers), cabin ambiance (92% of passengers), higher cabin humidity levels (80% of passengers), headroom (40% of passengers) and the larger windows (90% of passengers). 25% said they would go out of their way to again fly on the 787.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Other 787 operators have reported similar fuel savings, ranging from 20-22% compared with the 767-300ER.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup> An analysis by consulting firm AirInsight concluded that <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a>' 787s achieved an operating cost per seat that was 6% lower than the Airbus A330.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj-cut-cost_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-cut-cost-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>Early operators discovered that if the APS5000 <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">APU</a> was shut down with the inlet door closed, heat continued to build up in the tail compartment and cause the rotor shaft to bow. It could take up to 2 hours for the shaft to straighten again. This was particularly acute on short haul flights as there was insufficient time to allow the unit to cool before a restart was needed. Procedures were modified and the APU was later redesigned to address the issue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 15, 2012, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NTSB" title="NTSB">NTSB</a> requested the grounding of certain 787s due to GE engine failures; GE believed the production problem had been fixed by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902,_N26906_(8352837390).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="991" data-file-width="1600" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg/220px-United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg/330px-United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg/440px-United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902%2C_N26906_%288352837390%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:United_Dreamliner_Double_-_N26902,_N26906_(8352837390).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The second and third <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> 787-8s at <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_International_Airport" title="Los Angeles International Airport">Los Angeles International Airport</a>. United Airlines is the North American launch customer for all three 787 variants</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In March 2014, Mitsubishi Heavy Industries informed Boeing of a new problem that was caused by a change in manufacturing processes. Employees did not fill gaps with shims to connect wing rib aluminum shear ties to the carbon composite wing panels; the tightened fasteners, without shims, cause excessive stress that creates hairline cracks in the wings, which could enlarge and cause further damage. Forty-two aircraft awaiting delivery were affected, and each one required 1–2 weeks to inspect and repair. However, Boeing did not expect this problem to affect the overall delivery schedule, even if some airplanes were delivered late.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj-wing-defect_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-wing-defect-167">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>Dispatch reliability is an industry standard measure of the rate of departure from the gate with no more than 15 minutes delay due to technical issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> The 787-8 started out with a ~96% operational reliability, increasing to ~98.5% in April 2015. Daily utilization increased from five hours in 2013 to twelve hours in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>Airlines have often assigned the 787 to routes previously flown by larger aircraft that could not return a profit. For example, Air Canada offered a <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> to <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a> route, first utilizing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lockheed_L1011" title="Lockheed L1011">Lockheed L1011</a>, then a Boeing 747, then an <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">Airbus A340</a>, but none of these types were efficient enough to generate profit. The airline operated the route profitably with a 787-9, and credits the right number of seats and greater fuel efficiency for this success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Market_and_production_costs">Market and production costs</span></h3>
<p>The 787 Dreamliner program has reportedly cost Boeing $32 billion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sea_Times_cost_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sea_Times_cost-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>The cost of producing a 787 exceeded the purchase price at the end of 2013. Boeing's accounting method books sales immediately and distributes estimated production costs over ten years for the 1,300 aircraft it expects to deliver during that time. <a href="/wiki/JPMorgan_Chase" title="JPMorgan Chase">JPMorgan Chase</a> analyst Joseph Nadol estimated the program's cash loss to be $45 million per airplane, decreasing as the program moves forward. The actual cash flow reflects Boeing collecting most of the purchase price upon delivery; Boeing expects deferred costs to total $25 billion before the company begins to break even on production; the comparable number for the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>, adjusted for inflation, is $3.7 billion. Boeing plans to improve financial return by reorganizing the production line, renegotiating contracts with suppliers and labor unions, and increasing the 787 production rate, stepwise, to 12 airplanes per month by the end of 2016 and 14 airplanes per month by the end of the decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj-cut-cost_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-cut-cost-164">[164]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 787 program is expected to be profitable after 1,100 aircraft have been sold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-laing_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-laing-172">[172]</a></sup> As of April 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_787_Dreamliner&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the production rate is 10 per month;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LH150423_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-LH150423-173">[173]</a></sup> Boeing lost $30 million per 787 delivered in the first quarter of 2015, although Boeing plans to break even by the end of the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> The accumulated losses for the 787 totaled almost $27 billion by May 2015. The cost of producing the fuselage may increase because of a tentative deal reached with Spirit Aerosystems of <a href="/wiki/Wichita,_Kansas" title="Wichita, Kansas">Wichita, Kansas</a>, wherein severe price cuts demanded by Boeing would be eased, in return for a comprehensive agreement that lowers the cost of fuselages for other jetliners that Spirit helps Boeing manufacture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ150504_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ150504-175">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 21, 2016 Boeing reported charges of $847 million against two flight-test 787s built in 2009. Boeing had planned to refurbish and sell them, but instead wrote them off as research and development expense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design">Design</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2534" data-file-width="3378" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg/220px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg/330px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg/440px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_N787FT_KBFI.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A Boeing 787-8 taking off from <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Field" title="Boeing Field">Boeing Field</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_(G-ZBJC)_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3396" data-file-width="5753" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg/220px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg/330px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg/440px-British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28G-ZBJC%29_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:British_Airways_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_(G-ZBJC)_arrives_London_Heathrow_15Sep2015_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Front view of a <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> Boeing 787-8 arriving at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Airport" title="London Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a> (2015)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The design features light-weight construction. The aircraft is 80% <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite</a> by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> Boeing lists its materials by weight as 50% composite, 20% <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminum</a>, 15% <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">titanium</a>, 10% steel, and 5% other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_787_Fact_Sheet_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_787_Fact_Sheet-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005-179">[179]</a></sup> Aluminum is used for the wing and tail leading edges; titanium is used mainly on engines and fasteners, with steel used in various areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005_179-1"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>External features include a smooth nose contour, <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Raked_wingtip" title="Wingtip device">raked wingtips</a> and engine <a href="/wiki/Nacelle" title="Nacelle">nacelles</a> with noise-reducing serrated edges (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevron_(aerospace)" title="Chevron (aerospace)">chevrons</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noise_reducing_chevron_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-noise_reducing_chevron-180">[180]</a></sup> The longest-range 787 variant can fly 8,000 to 8,500 nautical miles (14,800 to 15,700 km), enough to cover the Los Angeles to <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a> or New York City to Hong Kong routes. Its cruising airspeed is <a href="/wiki/Mach_number" title="Mach number">Mach</a> 0.85,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_bg_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_bg-181">[181]</a></sup> equivalent to 561 mph (903 km/h; 487 kn) at typical cruise altitudes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flight_systems">Flight systems</span></h3>
<p>Among 787 flight systems, a key change from traditional airliners is the electrical architecture. The architecture is <a href="/wiki/Bleed_air#Bleedless_aircraft" title="Bleed air">bleedless</a> and replaces bleed air and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydraulic" title="Hydraulic">hydraulic</a> power sources with electrically powered compressors and pumps, while completely eliminating pneumatics and hydraulics from some subsystems, e.g., engine starters or brakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AERO_QTR_406_787_from_the_ground_up_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-AERO_QTR_406_787_from_the_ground_up-182">[182]</a></sup> Boeing says this system extracts 35% less power from the engines, allowing increased thrust and improved fuel economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobleed1_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobleed1-183">[183]</a></sup> The total available on-board electrical power is 1.45 megawatts, which is five times the power available on conventional pneumatic airliners;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-el-power_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-el-power-184">[184]</a></sup> the most notable electrically powered systems include: engine start, cabin pressurization, horizontal stabilizer trim, and wheel brakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Wing ice protection is another new system; it uses electro-thermal heater mats on the wing slats instead of traditional hot <a href="/wiki/Bleed_air" title="Bleed air">bleed air</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nobleed2_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-nobleed2-186">[186]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[187]</a></sup> An active gust alleviation system, similar to the system used on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B-2_Spirit" title="B-2 Spirit">B-2</a> bomber, improves ride quality during turbulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aero_America_200607_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aero_America_200607-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grumman_patent_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grumman_patent-189">[189]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="853" data-file-width="1280" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg/220px-Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg/330px-Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg/440px-Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_N787BA_cockpit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Boeing 787 flight deck</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787 has a "<a href="/wiki/Fly-by-wire" title="Fly-by-wire">fly-by-wire</a>" control system similar in architecture to that of the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup> The flight deck features LCD multi-function displays, which use an industry standard <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">Graphical user interface</a> <a href="/wiki/Widget_toolkit" title="Widget toolkit">widget toolkit</a> (Cockpit Display System Interfaces to User Systems / <a href="/wiki/ARINC_661" title="ARINC 661">ARINC 661</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whatisarinc661_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-whatisarinc661-191">[191]</a></sup> The 787 flight deck includes two <a href="/wiki/Head-up_display" title="Head-up display">head-up displays</a> (HUDs) as a standard feature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bca_20050831_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-bca_20050831-192">[192]</a></sup> The 787 shares a common <a href="/wiki/Type_rating" title="Type rating">type rating</a> with the larger 777, allowing qualified pilots to operate both models.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup> Like other Boeing airliners, the 787 uses a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yoke_(aircraft)" title="Yoke (aircraft)">yoke</a> instead of a <a href="/wiki/Side-stick" title="Side-stick">side-stick</a>. Under consideration is future integration of <a href="/wiki/Forward_looking_infrared" title="Forward looking infrared">forward looking infrared</a> into the HUD for thermal sensing, allowing pilots to "see" through clouds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-5"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orion_Multi-Purpose_Crew_Vehicle" title="Orion Multi-Purpose Crew Vehicle">Orion spacecraft</a> will use a glass cockpit derived from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honeywell_International" title="Honeywell International">Honeywell International</a>'s 787 flight deck systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fi_20061006_orion_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-fi_20061006_orion-194">[194]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Honeywell" title="Honeywell">Honeywell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rockwell_Collins" title="Rockwell Collins">Rockwell Collins</a> provide flight control, guidance, and other <a href="/wiki/Avionics" title="Avionics">avionics</a> systems, including standard dual <a href="/wiki/Head-up_display" title="Head-up display">head up guidance systems</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-6"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thales_Group" title="Thales Group">Thales</a> supplies the integrated standby flight display and power management,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-7"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Meggitt" title="Meggitt">Meggitt</a>/Securaplane provides the <a href="/wiki/Auxiliary_power_unit" title="Auxiliary power unit">auxiliary power unit</a> (APU) starting system, electrical power conversion system, and battery control system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meggitt_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-meggitt-195">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> with <a href="/wiki/Lithium_cobalt_oxide" title="Lithium cobalt oxide">lithium cobalt oxide</a> (LiCoO<sub>2</sub>) batteries by <a href="/wiki/GS_Yuasa" title="GS Yuasa">GS Yuasa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs2005_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-gs2005-198">[198]</a></sup> One of the two batteries weighs 28.5 kg and is rated 29.6 V, 76 Ah, giving 2.2 kWh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup> Battery charging is controlled by four independent systems to prevent overcharging following early lab testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> The battery systems are the focus of regulatory investigation due to multiple <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery#Safety" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium battery fires</a>, which led to <a href="#Groundings">grounding of the 787 fleet</a> starting in January 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faaground_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-faaground-201">[201]</a></sup></p>
<p>A version of <a href="/wiki/Ethernet" title="Ethernet">Ethernet</a> (<a href="/wiki/Avionics_Full-Duplex_Switched_Ethernet" title="Avionics Full-Duplex Switched Ethernet">Avionics Full-Duplex Switched Ethernet</a> (AFDX) / <a href="/wiki/ARINC" title="ARINC">ARINC</a> 664) transmits data between the flight deck and aircraft systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miltary_Electronics_200607_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miltary_Electronics_200607-202">[202]</a></sup> The control, navigation, and communication systems are networked with the passenger cabin's in-flight internet systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_May_Be_Vulnerable_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_May_Be_Vulnerable-203">[203]</a></sup> In January 2008, FAA concerns were reported regarding possible passenger access to the 787's computer networks; Boeing has stated that various protective hardware and software solutions are employed, including <a href="/wiki/Air_gap_(networking)" title="Air gap (networking)">air gaps</a> to physically separate the networks, and <a href="/wiki/Firewall_(computing)" title="Firewall (computing)">firewalls</a> for software separation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_May_Be_Vulnerable_203-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_May_Be_Vulnerable-203">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> These measures prevent data transfer from the passenger internet system to the maintenance or navigation systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_May_Be_Vulnerable_203-2"><a href="#cite_note-787_May_Be_Vulnerable-203">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Composite_materials">Composite materials</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:787fuselage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="334" data-file-width="500" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/787fuselage.jpg/220px-787fuselage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/787fuselage.jpg/330px-787fuselage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/787fuselage.jpg/440px-787fuselage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:787fuselage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Disassembled composite fuselage section of the Boeing 787</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787 is the first major commercial airplane to have a composite fuselage, composite wings, and use composites in most other airframe components.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Composites_flying_prt1_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Composites_flying_prt1-205">[205]</a></sup> Each 787 contains approximately 77,000 pounds (35 metric tons) of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carbon-fiber-reinforced_polymer" title="Carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer">carbon-fiber-reinforced polymer</a> (CFRP), made with 51,000 lb (23 t) of carbon fiber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Toray_2005_0412_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Toray_2005_0412-206">[206]</a></sup> Carbon fiber composites have a higher <a href="/wiki/Specific_strength" title="Specific strength">strength-to-weight ratio</a> than conventional aircraft materials, and help make the 787 a lighter aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005_179-2"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005-179">[179]</a></sup> Composites are used on fuselage, wings, tail, doors, and interior.
