Boeing had built and tested the first commercial aircraft composite section while studying the proposed <a href="/wiki/Boeing_Sonic_Cruiser" title="Boeing Sonic Cruiser">Sonic Cruiser</a> in the early 2000s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bca_scfuse_test_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-bca_scfuse_test-207">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bca_787_first_s41_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-bca_787_first_s41-208">[208]</a></sup> The first carbon/epoxy primary structure was put into service on the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737_Classic" title="Boeing 737 Classic">Boeing 737 Classic</a> horizontal tail in 1984, and the largest use of composite structures is 60% in the <a href="/wiki/Boeing%E2%80%93Sikorsky_RAH-66_Comanche" title="Boeing–Sikorsky RAH-66 Comanche">Boeing–Sikorsky RAH-66 Comanche</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></p>
<p>Carbon fiber, unlike metal, does not visibly show cracks and fatigue, prompting concerns about the safety risks of widespread use of the material;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattletimes_09_18_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-seattletimes_09_18-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bizweek_20060619_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-bizweek_20060619-211">[211]</a></sup> the rival <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A350_XWB" title="Airbus A350 XWB">Airbus A350 XWB</a> uses composite panels on a frame, a more conventional approach, which its contractors regarded as less risky.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drops_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-drops-80">[80]</a></sup> Although fired in 2006, Boeing engineer <a href="/wiki/Vince_Weldon" title="Vince Weldon">Vince Weldon</a> complained to management, and later to the public: the composite fuselage was unsafe compared to conventional aluminum designs, and in a crash, was more likely to "shatter too easily and burn with toxic fumes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-register20070919_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-register20070919-212">[212]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, a potential issue is the porous nature of composite materials: collected moisture expanding with altitude can cause <a href="/wiki/Delamination" title="Delamination">delamination</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Star_Telegram_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Star_Telegram-213">[213]</a></sup> Boeing responded that composites have been used on wings and other passenger aircraft parts for many years without incident, and special defect detection procedures will be instituted for the 787 to detect any potential hidden damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattlepi_20060110_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattlepi_20060110-214">[214]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2006, Boeing launched the 787 GoldCare program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boeingfrontiers_200607_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-boeingfrontiers_200607-215">[215]</a></sup> This is an optional, comprehensive life-cycle management service, whereby aircraft in the program are routinely monitored and repaired, as needed. Although the first program of its kind from Boeing, post-sale protection programs are not new; such programs are usually offered by third party service centers. Boeing is also designing and testing composite hardware so inspections are mainly visual. This reduces the need for ultrasonic and other non-visual inspection methods, saving time and money.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Engines">Engines</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg/220px-Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg/330px-Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg/440px-Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toothed_exhaust_duct_covers_on_a_Boeing_787.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The chevron-toothed exhaust duct covers on the first 787, shown here with thrust-reversers deployed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787 is powered by two engines; these engines use all-electrical <a href="/wiki/Bleed_air" title="Bleed air">bleedless</a> systems, eliminating the superheated air conduits normally used for aircraft power, de-icing, and other functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-8"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> As part of its "Quiet Technology Demonstrator 2" project, Boeing adopted several engine noise-reducing technologies for the 787. These include an air inlet containing sound-absorbing materials and exhaust duct cover with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevron_(aerospace)" title="Chevron (aerospace)">chevron</a>-toothed pattern on the rim for a quieter mixing of exhaust and outside air.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-noise_reducing_chevron_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-noise_reducing_chevron-180">[180]</a></sup> Boeing expects these developments to make the 787 significantly quieter both inside and out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Goodrich_noise_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Goodrich_noise-217">[217]</a></sup> The noise-reducing measures prevent sounds above 85 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a> from leaving airport boundaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005_179-3"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005-179">[179]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two different engine models compatible with the 787 use a standard electrical interface to allow an aircraft to be fitted with either <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_plc" title="Rolls-Royce plc">Rolls-Royce</a> <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_1000" title="Rolls-Royce Trent 1000">Trent 1000</a> or <a href="/wiki/GE_Aviation" title="GE Aviation">General Electric</a> <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GEnx" title="General Electric GEnx">GEnx</a> engines. This interchangeability aims to save time and cost when changing engine types;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_flyingredef_6-9"><a href="#cite_note-787_flyingredef-6">[6]</a></sup> while previous aircraft could exchange engines for those of a different manufacturer, the high cost and time required made it rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-What.27s_new_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-What.27s_new-218">[218]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leeham_20060718_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-leeham_20060718-219">[219]</a></sup> In 2006, Boeing addressed reports of an extended change period by stating that the 787 engine swap was intended to take 24 hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leeham_20060718_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-leeham_20060718-219">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2016, Rolls Royce began flight testing its new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trent_1000" title="Trent 1000">Trent 1000 TEN</a> engine. It has a new compressor system based on the compressor in <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Trent_XWB" title="Rolls-Royce Trent XWB">Rolls-Royce Trent XWB</a> engine and a new turbine design for extra thrust, up to 78,000 lbf (350 kN). Rolls Royce plans to offer the TEN on the 787-8, -9 and -10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interior">Interior</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner,_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="683" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg/220px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg/330px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg/440px-Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner%2C_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner,_All_Nippon_Airways_-_ANA_AN2287223.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ANA's first 787 Dreamliner with 2–4–2 <a href="/wiki/Economy_class" title="Economy class">economy class</a> seating</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787-8 is designed to typically seat 234 passengers in a three-class setup, 240 in two-class domestic configuration, and 296 passengers in a high-density economy arrangement. Seat rows can be arranged in four to seven abreast in first or business (e.g., 1–2–1, 2–2–2, 2-3-2). Eight or nine abreast are options in economy (e.g., 3–2–3, 2–4–2, 3–3–3). Typical seat room ranges from 46 to 61 in (120 to 150 cm) pitch in first, 36 to 39 in (91 to 99 cm) in business, and 32 to 34 in (81 to 86 cm) in economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_Airport_descrip_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-787_Airport_descrip-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Cabin interior width is approximately 18 feet (550 cm) at armrest level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_Airport_descrip_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_Airport_descrip-221">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spi_787_details_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-spi_787_details-223">[223]</a></sup> The Dreamliner's cabin width is 15 inches (38 cm) more than that of the Airbus A330 and A340,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup> 5 inches (13 cm) less than the A350,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> and 16 in (41 cm) less than the 777.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup> The 787's economy seats can be up to 17.5 in (44.4 cm) wide for nine-abreast seating<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> and up to 19 inches (48 cm) wide for eight-abreast seating arrangements. Most airlines are selecting the nine-abreast (3–3–3) configuration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wide_228-0"><a href="#cite_note-wide-228">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> The 787's nine-abreast seating for economy provides passengers less space, particularly across the hips and shoulders, than any other jet airliner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runwaygirl1_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-runwaygirl1-230">[230]</a></sup> This has led to recommendations that passengers, particularly taller or larger individuals, avoid the 787 for international service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runwaygirl1_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-runwaygirl1-230">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-runwaygirl2_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-runwaygirl2-232">[232]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg"><img alt="787 cabin. It shows the 787's spacious cabin. Above the blue seats are overhead bins and a rainbow light effect." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg/220px-ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg/330px-ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg/440px-ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ANA_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_cabin_LED_show.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The 787's larger windows are designed to improve passenger views.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787's cabin windows are larger than any other civil air transport in-service or in development,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787wind_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-787wind-233">[233]</a></sup> with dimensions of 10.7 by 18.4 in (27 by 47 cm),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787wind_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-787wind-233">[233]</a></sup> and a higher eye level so passengers can maintain a view of the horizon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windows_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-windows-234">[234]</a></sup> The composite fuselage permits larger windows without the need for structural reinforcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200949_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200949-235">[235]</a></sup> Instead of plastic window shades, the windows use <a href="/wiki/Electrochromism" title="Electrochromism">electrochromism</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Smart_glass" title="Smart glass">smart glass</a> (supplied by <a href="/wiki/PPG_Industries" title="PPG Industries">PPG Industries</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fi_20051220_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-fi_20051220-236">[236]</a></sup> allowing flight attendants<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scho_237-0"><a href="#cite_note-scho-237">[237]</a></sup> and passengers to adjust five levels of sunlight and visibility to their liking,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-brown-238">[238]</a></sup> reducing cabin glare while maintaining a view to the outside world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-windows_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-windows-234">[234]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turner220_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-turner220-239">[239]</a></sup> but the most opaque setting still has some transparency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scho_237-1"><a href="#cite_note-scho-237">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> The lavatory, however, has a traditional sunshade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-brown_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-brown-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 787's cabin features <a href="/wiki/Light-emitting_diode" title="Light-emitting diode">light-emitting diodes</a> (LEDs)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LED_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-LED-241">[241]</a></sup> as standard equipment, allowing the aircraft to be entirely 'bulbless'. LED lights have previously been an option on the Boeing 777 and Airbus aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-diehl_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-diehl-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> The system has three-color LEDs plus a white LED.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LED_241-1"><a href="#cite_note-LED-241">[241]</a></sup> The 787 interior was designed to better accommodate persons with mobility, sensory, and cognitive disabilities. For example, a 56 by 57 in (140 by 140 cm) convertible lavatory includes a movable center wall that allows two separate lavatories to become one large, wheelchair-accessible facility.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_787_Improved_Access_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_787_Improved_Access-244">[244]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1200" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg/220px-Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg/330px-Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg/440px-Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Business_class_of_Qatar_Airways%27_25th_Boeing_787-8.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> 787-8 <a href="/wiki/Business_class" title="Business class">business class</a> cabin in 1-2-1 layout</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The internal cabin pressure of the 787 is increased to the equivalent of 6,000 feet (1,800 m) altitude instead of the 8,000 feet (2,400 m) on older conventional aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-breathe_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-breathe-245">[245]</a></sup> According to Boeing, in a joint study with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oklahoma_State_University" title="Oklahoma State University">Oklahoma State University</a>, this will significantly improve passenger comfort.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aero_America_200607_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aero_America_200607-188">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bca_20040719_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-bca_20040719-246">[246]</a></sup> Cabin air pressurization is provided by electrically driven compressors, rather than traditional engine-bleed air, thereby eliminating the need to cool heated air before it enters the cabin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Design_News_20070604_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-Design_News_20070604-247">[247]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> The cabin's humidity is programmable based on the number of passengers carried, and allows 15% humidity settings instead of the 4% found in previous aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-breathe_245-1"><a href="#cite_note-breathe-245">[245]</a></sup> The composite fuselage avoids metal fatigue issues associated with higher cabin pressure, and eliminates the risk of corrosion from higher humidity levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-breathe_245-2"><a href="#cite_note-breathe-245">[245]</a></sup> The cabin air-conditioning system improves air quality by removing ozone from outside air, and besides standard <a href="/wiki/HEPA" title="HEPA">HEPA</a> filters which remove <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">airborne particles</a>, uses a gaseous filtration system to remove odors, irritants, and gaseous contaminants as well as particulates like viruses, bacteria and allergens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005_179-4"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_AIAA_May_2005-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-turner220_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-turner220-239">[239]</a></sup> The bleedless engine cabin air system also allows the 787 air to avoid oil fumes and toxins which are dangerous to the health of passengers and crew and are found in all other aircraft bleed air systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Variants">Variants</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg"><img alt="Diagrams of outlines of three different aircraft imposed over one another." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="425" data-file-width="1025" height="91" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg/220px-Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg/330px-Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg/440px-Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_787_size_comparison.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Size comparison of the Boeing 787-8 (<b>black outline</b>) with the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777-300</a> (<span style="background:#d3d3d3">gray</span>), <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">767-300</a> (<span style="background:#00ced1">teal</span>), and <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">737-800</a> (<span style="color:#fff; background:#4040ff;">blue</span>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Boeing has offered four passenger variants since the 787 program launch in 2004; three are being or will be built. The 787-8 was the first variant produced, and was followed by the 787-9 in 2014, with the 787-10 at a later date. A short-range model, the 787-3, was originally offered for sale but development was cancelled. The <a href="/wiki/International_Civil_Aviation_Organization" title="International Civil Aviation Organization">International Civil Aviation Organization</a> (ICAO) aircraft type designator system classifies the 787-8 and 787-9 under the codes "B788" and "B789", respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICAOcode_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICAOcode-250">[250]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="787-3">787-3</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="750" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg/220px-Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg/330px-Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg/440px-Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_rendering_787-3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An artist's impression of the 787-3, which would have featured a shorter wing with winglets.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The 787-3 was targeted for high-density flights; it was designed as a 290-seat (two-class) short-range version with a fully loaded range of 2,500 to 3,050 nautical miles (4,650 to 5,650 km). Using the same basic fuselage as the 787-8, the wing was derived from the 787-8, with blended <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Winglet" title="Wingtip device">winglets</a> instead of <a href="/wiki/Wingtip_device#Raked_wingtip" title="Wingtip device">raked wingtips</a>. The change decreased the wingspan by roughly 25 feet (7.6 m), allowing the -3 to fit more domestic gates, particularly in Japan. This variant would have been limited in range by a reduced MTOW of 364,000 lb (165,000 kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787-3_specs_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-787-3_specs-251">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[note 1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The variant was designed to operate on <a href="/wiki/Boeing_757" title="Boeing 757">Boeing 757-300</a>/<a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">767-200</a>-sized regional routes from airports with restricted gate spacing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200938_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisWagner200938-253">[252]</a></sup> Boeing projected the future of aviation as between very large, but close, cities, of five million or more people; city populations may stabilize around the capacity level of the 787-3.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baseler__Randy_200505_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baseler_Randy_200505-254">[253]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FAA_2005_20704_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-FAA_2005_20704-255">[254]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two Japanese airlines ordered 45 Boeing 787-3s; however, production problems on the base 787-8 model led Boeing, in April 2008, to postpone the introduction of the -3 until after the 787-9's introduction, but without a firm delivery date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_variants_delayed_to_at_least_2012_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-787_variants_delayed_to_at_least_2012-68">[68]</a></sup> By January 2010, all 787-3 orders had been converted to the 787-8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[255]</a></sup> The 787-3 experienced a lack of interest by potential customers because it was designed specifically for the Japanese market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Boeing_likely_scrap_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Boeing_likely_scrap-257">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[257]</a></sup> Boeing canceled the 787-3 in December 2010 because it was no longer financially viable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[258]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="787-8">787-8</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2425" data-file-width="3637" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg/220px-Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg/330px-Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg/440px-Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Thomson_Airways_Boeing_787_G-TUIB.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thomson_Airways" title="Thomson Airways">Thomson Airways</a> 787-8 at Hannover Airport in June 2013</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With a typical capacity of 242 passengers and a range of 7,355 nautical miles (8,464 mi; 13,621 km), the -8 is the base model of the 787 family and was the first to enter service in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-characteristics_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-characteristics-260">[259]</a></sup> The 787-8 is targeted to replace the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_767" title="Boeing 767">Boeing 767</a>-200ER and -300ER, as well as expand into new non-stop markets where larger planes would not be economically viable. Approximately 35% of 787 orders are for the 787-8 in December 2016, with 324 delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-787_O_D_summ-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="787-9">787-9</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="682" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/220px-Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/330px-Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg/440px-Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air_New_Zealand_Boeing_787-9_Dreamliner_landing_at_Perth_Airport.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The first 787-9 in service, operated by launch customer <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a>, landing at the inaugural destination, <a href="/wiki/Perth_Airport" title="Perth Airport">Perth Airport</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Keeping the same wingspan as the 787-8, the 787-9 is a lengthened and strengthened variant with a 20 feet (6.1 m) longer fuselage and a 54,500 pounds (24,700 kg) higher maximum take-off weight (MTOW), seating 280 passengers in a typical three-class arrangement over a 7,635 nautical miles (8,786 mi; 14,140 km) range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-characteristics_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-characteristics-260">[259]</a></sup> It features <a href="/wiki/Boundary_layer_control" title="Boundary layer control">active boundary-layer control</a> on the tail surfaces, reducing drag.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king1_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-king1-261">[260]</a></sup> Boeing is targeting the 787-9 to replace its own <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boeing_767-400ER" title="Boeing 767-400ER">767-400ER</a>, to compete with both passenger variants of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a> and to allow opening new long routes like the 787-8.</p>
<p>In 2005, the entry into service (EIS) was planned for 2010. The firm configuration was finalized on July 1, 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[261]</a></sup> By October 2011, deliveries were scheduled to begin in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[262]</a></sup></p>
<p>The prototype 787-9 made its maiden flight from Paine Field on September 17, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[263]</a></sup> A 787-9 was on static display at the 2014 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Farnborough_Air_Show" title="Farnborough Air Show">Farnborough Air Show</a> prior to first delivery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[264]</a></sup> On July 8, 2014, Launch customer <a href="/wiki/Air_New_Zealand" title="Air New Zealand">Air New Zealand</a> took its first 787-9, in a distinctive black livery in a ceremony at Paine Field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-266"><a href="#cite_note-266">[265]</a></sup> Its first commercial flight was from <a href="/wiki/Auckland_Airport" title="Auckland Airport">Auckland</a> to Sydney on August 9, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ANZ_first_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-ANZ_first-267">[266]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 787-9 was to begin commercial service with <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> on August 7, 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[267]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> was to start the longest nonstop scheduled 787 service between Los Angeles and Melbourne in October 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[268]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Air_China" title="Air China">Air China</a> started a 787-9 route between Beijing and Chengdu in May 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[269]</a></sup> As of December 2016, 53% of all 787 orders are for the 787-9, with 176 deliveries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-787_O_D_summ-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 20-ft stretch was achieved by adding a 10-ft (five-frame) extension forward and aft. The 787-8 and 787-9 have 50% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonality" title="Commonality">commonality</a>: the wing, fuselage and systems of the 787-8 had required radical revision to achieve the <a href="/wiki/Payload" title="Payload">payload</a>-range goals of the 787-9. Following a major revamp of the original 787-8 wing, the latest configuration for the 787-9 and -10 is the fourth design evolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW161219_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-AW161219-271">[270]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="787-10">787-10</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="750" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg/220px-Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg/330px-Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg/440px-Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_render_787-10.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's impression of the 787-10, the largest Dreamliner variant</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In December 2005, pushed by the interest of <a href="/wiki/Emirates_(airline)" title="Emirates (airline)">Emirates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Qantas" title="Qantas">Qantas</a>, Boeing was studying the possibility of stretching the 787-9 further to seat 290 to 310 passengers and named 787-10. This variant would be similar to the capacity of the <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A350_XWB" title="Airbus A350 XWB">Airbus A350-900</a> and Boeing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/777-200ER" title="777-200ER">777-200ER</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[271]</a></sup> Customers discussions were continuing in early 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baseler_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-baseler-273">[272]</a></sup> Mike Bair, Boeing's vice president and general manager for the 787 development program at the time, said it was easier to proceed with the 787-10 development after other customers followed Emirates' request.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_20060327_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj_20060327-274">[273]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 30, 2013, <a href="/wiki/Singapore_Airlines" title="Singapore Airlines">Singapore Airlines</a> became the launch customer by stating it would order 30 of the 787-10, provided Boeing launches the program, to be delivered in 2018–2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[274]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[275]</a></sup> On June 18, 2013, Boeing officially launched the 787-10 at the Paris Air Show, with orders or commitments for 102 aircraft from <a href="/wiki/Air_Lease_Corporation" title="Air Lease Corporation">Air Lease Corporation</a> (30), Singapore Airlines (30), <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> (20), <a href="/wiki/British_Airways" title="British Airways">British Airways</a> (IAG) (12), and <a href="/wiki/GE_Capital_Aviation_Services" title="GE Capital Aviation Services">GE Capital Aviation Services</a> (10).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787-10_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-787-10-277">[276]</a></sup></p>
<p>The 787-10 is to be 224 ft 1 in (68.30 m) long, seat 330 passengers in a two-class cabin configuration, and have a range of 6,430 nmi (11,910 km; 7,400 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> The variant was envisioned as replacing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/777-200" title="777-200">777-200</a>, <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A330" title="Airbus A330">Airbus A330</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">A340</a> aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[278]</a></sup> The -10 is to compete against the A350-900, and offer better economics than the Airbus on shorter routes, according to Boeing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[279]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steven_Udvar-Hazy" title="Steven Udvar-Hazy">Steven Udvar-Hazy</a> said "If it's identically configured, the -10 has a little bit of an edge on the -900", but smaller than Boeing's estimate of 10 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[280]</a></sup> Boeing completed detailed design for the -10 on December 2, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[281]</a></sup> Boeing began major assembly in March 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[282]</a></sup> Major fuselage parts were received for final assembly on November 30, 2016, first flight is planned for 2017; built only in Charleston, South Carolina, first delivery is planned for 2018 to Singapore Airlines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[283]</a></sup></p>
<p>Designers targeted 90% <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commonality" title="Commonality">commonality</a> between the 787-9 and -10 and achieved 95%; the 18-ft stretch was reached by adding 10 ft forward and 8 ft aft, and by strengthening the fuselage for bending loads in the center <a href="/wiki/Wingbox" title="Wingbox">wingbox</a>. Because of the length and additional <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tail_strike" title="Tail strike">tail strike</a> protection needed, a semilevered <a href="/wiki/Landing_gear" title="Landing gear">landing gear</a> enables rotation over the aft wheels rather than at a the bogie center, like the 777-300ER, and cabin <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> has 15% more capacity. The first and third -10s will have Rolls-Royce’s new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trent_1000" title="Trent 1000">Trent 1000</a> TEN engines, while the second will be powered by the competing General Electric <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GEnx" title="GEnx">GEnx</a>-1B.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AW161219_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-AW161219-271">[270]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Further_proposals">Further proposals</span></h3>
<p>Although with no set date, Boeing expects to build, possibly in the 2018-2023 timeframe, a 787 freighter version.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[284]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[285]</a></sup> Boeing also reportedly considered a 787 variant as a candidate to replace the 747-based <a href="/wiki/Boeing_VC-25" title="Boeing VC-25">VC-25</a> presidential transport in 2009;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[286]</a></sup> this was unlikely as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a> has traditionally used aircraft proven in service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[287]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Operators">Operators</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_787_operators" title="List of Boeing 787 operators">List of Boeing 787 operators</a></div>
<p>There were 423 Boeing 787 aircraft in airline service as of July 2016<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Boeing_787_Dreamliner&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, comprising 299 787-8s and 124 787-9s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FI16_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-FI16-289">[288]</a></sup> The largest operators were <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> (47), <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> (30), <a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">Japan Airlines</a> (29), <a href="/wiki/Qatar_Airways" title="Qatar Airways">Qatar Airways</a> (29), <a href="/wiki/LATAM_Chile" title="LATAM Chile">LATAM Chile</a> (formerly LAN Airlines) (22), <a href="/wiki/Air_India" title="Air India">Air India</a> (21), <a href="/wiki/Air_Canada" title="Air Canada">Air Canada</a> (21), and other airlines operating fewer of the type.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FI16_289-1"><a href="#cite_note-FI16-289">[288]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orders_and_deliveries">Orders and deliveries</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Boeing_787_orders_and_deliveries" title="List of Boeing 787 orders and deliveries">List of Boeing 787 orders and deliveries</a></div>

</div>
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_(VT-ANG)_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1566" data-file-width="2384" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg/220px-Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg/330px-Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg/440px-Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_%28VT-ANG%29_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Air_India_Boeing_787-8_Dreamliner_(VT-ANG)_departs_London_Heathrow_Airport_2ndJuly2014_arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Air_India" title="Air India">Air India</a> Boeing 787-8 Dreamliner departs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Heathrow_Airport" title="London Heathrow Airport">London Heathrow Airport</a>, 2014</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In September 2011, the 787 was first officially delivered to launch customer <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[290]</a></sup> As of December 2016, the top three identified customers for the 787 are: All Nippon Airways with 83 orders (36 -8s, 44 -9s and three -10s), <a href="/wiki/International_Lease_Finance_Corporation" title="International Lease Finance Corporation">ILFC</a> (an aircraft leasing company), with orders totaling 74 Boeing 787s (26 -8s and 48 -9s), and Etihad Airways with 71 orders (41 -9s and 30 -10s).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-4"><a href="#cite_note-787_O_D_summ-1">[1]</a></sup></p>

<p><br/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Accidents_and_notable_incidents">Accidents and notable incidents</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="754" data-file-width="1134" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg/220px-Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg/330px-Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg/440px-Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dreamliner_battery_fire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Japan Airlines 787 Dreamliner at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Logan_Airport" title="Boston Logan Airport">Boston Logan Airport</a> with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithium_ion_battery#Safety" title="Lithium ion battery">lithium-ion battery fire</a> and resulting heavy smoke coming out a cargo hold</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3341" data-file-width="5012" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg/220px-ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg/330px-ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg/440px-ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ANA_787s_grounded_at_HND.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Three 787s of All Nippon Airways sit grounded at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokyo_International_Airport" title="Tokyo International Airport">Tokyo Haneda International Airport</a> in late January 2013.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Boeing 787 has been involved in 1 <a href="/wiki/Aviation_accidents_and_incidents" title="Aviation accidents and incidents">aviation incident</a>. In December 2012, Boeing CEO James McNerney stated that the problems were no greater than those experienced with the introduction of other models such as the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_777" title="Boeing 777">Boeing 777</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-July_2013_Incident_Timeline_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-July_2013_Incident_Timeline-297">[296]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Operational_problems">Operational problems</span></h3>
<p>A Japan Airlines (JAL) 787 experienced a fuel leak on January 8, 2013, and its flight from Boston was canceled.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[297]</a></sup> On January 9, <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines" title="United Airlines">United Airlines</a> reported a problem in one of its six 787s with the wiring near the main batteries. After these incidents, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Transportation_Safety_Board" title="National Transportation Safety Board">National Transportation Safety Board</a> subsequently opened a safety probe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[298]</a></sup> Later, on January 11, 2013, another aircraft was found to have a fuel leak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[299]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also on January 11, 2013, the FAA completed a comprehensive review of the 787's critical systems, including the design, manufacture and assembly; the Department of Transportation secretary <a href="/wiki/Ray_LaHood" title="Ray LaHood">Ray LaHood</a> stated the administration was "looking for the root causes" behind the recent issues. The head of the FAA, Michael Huerta, said that so far nothing found "suggests [the 787] is not safe".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[300]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 13, 2013, a JAL 787 at <a href="/wiki/Narita_International_Airport" title="Narita International Airport">Narita International Airport</a> outside <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> was found to also have a fuel leak during an inspection, the third time a fuel leak had been reported within a week. The aircraft reportedly was the same one that had a fuel leak in Boston on January 8.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[301]</a></sup> This leak was caused by a different valve; the causes of the leaks are unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[302]</a></sup> Japan's transport ministry has also launched an investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[303]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 12, 2013, a fire started on an empty <a href="/wiki/Ethiopian_Airlines" title="Ethiopian Airlines">Ethiopian Airlines</a> 787 parked at <a href="/wiki/Heathrow_Airport" title="Heathrow Airport">Heathrow Airport</a> before it was extinguished by the airport fire and rescue service. No injuries were reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_Ethiopian_fire_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_Ethiopian_fire-305">[304]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-306"><a href="#cite_note-306">[305]</a></sup> The fire caused extensive heat damage to the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-307"><a href="#cite_note-307">[306]</a></sup> The FAA and NTSB sent representatives to assist in the investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[307]</a></sup> The initial investigation found no direct link with the aircraft's main batteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Batteries_.27not_linked.27_to_787_fire_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-Batteries_.27not_linked.27_to_787_fire-309">[308]</a></sup> Further investigations indicated that the fire was due to lithium-manganese dioxide batteries powering an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distress_radiobeacon" title="Distress radiobeacon">emergency locator transmitter</a> (ELT).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[309]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[310]</a></sup> The UK <a href="/wiki/Air_Accidents_Investigation_Branch" title="Air Accidents Investigation Branch">Air Accidents Investigation Branch</a> (AAIB) issued a special bulletin on July 18, 2013 requesting the US FAA ensure that the locator is removed or disconnected in Boeing 787s, and to review the safety of lithium battery-powered ELT systems in other aircraft types.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-312"><a href="#cite_note-312">[311]</a></sup> On August 19, 2015, the Associated Press reported that the fire was started by a short circuit, caused by crossed wires located under the battery. The Air Accidents Investigation Branch's investigators recommended that "the U.S. Federal Aviation Administration, together with similar bodies in Europe and Canada, should conduct a review of equipment powered by lithium metal batteries to ensure they have 'an acceptable level of circuit protection.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[312]</a></sup></p>
<p>On July 26, 2013, ANA said it had found wiring damage on two 787 locator beacons. United Airlines also reported that it had found a pinched wire in one 787 locator beacon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[313]</a></sup> On August 14, 2013, the media reported a fire extinguisher fault affecting three ANA airplanes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fire_Extinguisher_Fault_315-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fire_Extinguisher_Fault-315">[314]</a></sup> which was caused by a supplier assembly error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[315]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 28, 2013, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Long_Haul" title="Norwegian Long Haul">Norwegian Long Haul</a> decided to take one of its two 787s in its fleet at the time out of service after the two aircraft broke down on more than six occasions in September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[316]</a></sup> The company will lease an <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">Airbus A340</a> for its long-haul operations while the 787 is returned to Boeing for repair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[317]</a></sup> On December 20–22, 2013, Norwegian Long Haul experienced technical problems keeping two of its three 787 aircraft grounded at Fort Lauderdale airport and delayed six flights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[318]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[319]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 22, 2013, Boeing issued an advisory to airlines using <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GEnx" title="General Electric GEnx">General Electric GEnx</a> engines on 787 and 747-8 aircraft to avoid flying near high-level <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thunderstorms" title="Thunderstorms">thunderstorms</a> due to an increased risk of icing on the engines. The problem was caused by a buildup of ice crystals just behind the main fan, causing a brief loss of thrust on six occasions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[320]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 21, 2014, a <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_Air_Shuttle" title="Norwegian Air Shuttle">Norwegian Air Shuttle</a> 787 experienced a fuel leak which caused a 19-hour delay to a flight from Bangkok to Oslo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek1_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-322">[321]</a></sup> Footage of the leak taken by passengers show fuel gushing out of the left wing of the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[322]</a></sup> The leak became known to pilots only after it was pointed out by concerned passengers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[323]</a></sup> It was found later that a faulty valve was responsible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[324]</a></sup> This fuel leak is one of numerous problems experienced by Norwegian Air Shuttle's 787 fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-businessweek1_322-1"><a href="#cite_note-businessweek1-322">[321]</a></sup> Mike Fleming, Boeing's vice president for 787 support and services, subsequently met with executives of Norwegian Air Shuttle and expressed Boeing's commitment to improving the 787's dispatch reliability, "we’re not satisfied with where the airplane is today, flying at a fleet average of 98 percent... The 777 today flies at 99.4 percent ... and that's the benchmark that the 787 needs to attain”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-327"><a href="#cite_note-327">[326]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 24, 2015, Indian media reported that an Air India 787 Dreamliner (VT-AND) had been grounded since January 2015 and had been scavenged for parts due to their lack of availability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-328"><a href="#cite_note-328">[327]</a></sup> Air India's aircraft engineers' body advised against accepting further deliveries until Boeing resolved reliability issues. India's Minister of State for Civil Aviation Mahesh Sharma stated the reliability issues to India's Parliament.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-329"><a href="#cite_note-329">[328]</a></sup></p>
<p>On March 4, 2016, Ethiopian Airlines 787-8 registration ET-ASH performing Flight ET-702 from Addis Ababa, Ethiopia to Rome Fiumicino, Italy, had its nose gear collapse before flight was ready to depart. A flight attendant received minor injuries and the aircraft was damaged.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-330"><a href="#cite_note-330">[329]</a></sup></p>
<p>Later in March 2016 the FAA accelerated the release of an airworthiness directive in response to reports indicating that in certain weather conditions "erroneous low airspeed may be displayed ..." There was concern "abrupt pilot control inputs in this condition could exceed the structural capability of the airplane." Pilots were told not to apply "large, abrupt control column inputs" in the event of an "unrealistic" drop in displayed airspeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-331"><a href="#cite_note-331">[330]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[331]</a></sup></p>
<p>On April 22, 2016, the FAA issued an airworthiness directive following a January 29 incident in which a <a href="/wiki/General_Electric_GEnx" title="General Electric GEnx">General Electric GEnx</a>-1B PIP2 engine suffered damage and non-restartable power loss while flying at an altitude of 20,000 feet. The damage is thought to have been caused by a fan imbalance resulting from fan ice shedding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[333]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[334]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Battery_problems">Battery problems</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Boeing_787_Dreamliner_battery_problems" title="Boeing 787 Dreamliner battery problems">Boeing 787 Dreamliner battery problems</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2560" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG/220px-Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG/330px-Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG/440px-Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mike_Bauer_787_010813.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Aft Electronics Bay that held the JAL 787 battery that caught fire</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="684" data-file-width="1088" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg/220px-Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg/330px-Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg/440px-Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Boeing_Dreamliner_battery_original_and_damaged.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Japan Airlines 787 battery comparison; Left: typical original battery. Right: damaged battery.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On January 16, 2013, <a href="/wiki/All_Nippon_Airways" title="All Nippon Airways">All Nippon Airways</a> Flight NH-692, en route from <a href="/wiki/Yamaguchi_Ube_Airport" title="Yamaguchi Ube Airport">Yamaguchi Ube Airport</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokyo_Haneda" title="Tokyo Haneda">Tokyo Haneda</a>, had a battery problem warning followed by a burning smell while climbing from Ube about 35 nautical miles (65 km) west of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Takamatsu,_Japan" title="Takamatsu, Japan">Takamatsu, Japan</a>. The aircraft diverted to Takamatsu and was evacuated via the slides; three passengers received minor injuries during the evacuation. Inspection revealed a battery fire. A similar incident in a parked <a href="/wiki/Japan_Airlines" title="Japan Airlines">Japan Airlines</a> 787 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston,_Massachusetts" title="Boston, Massachusetts">Boston</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Logan_International_Airport" title="Logan International Airport">Logan International Airport</a> within the same week led the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Aviation_Administration" title="Federal Aviation Administration">Federal Aviation Administration</a> to ground all 787s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avherald1_336-0"><a href="#cite_note-avherald1-336">[335]</a></sup> On January 16, 2013, both major Japanese airlines ANA and JAL voluntarily grounded their fleets of 787s after multiple incidents involving different 787s, including emergency landings. At the time, these two carriers operated 24 of the 50 787s delivered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[336]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[337]</a></sup> The grounding reportedly cost ANA some 9 billion yen (US$93 million) in lost sales.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-339"><a href="#cite_note-339">[338]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 16, 2013, the FAA issued an <a href="/wiki/Emergency_airworthiness_directive" title="Emergency airworthiness directive">emergency airworthiness directive</a> ordering all American-based airlines to ground their Boeing 787s until yet-to-be-determined modifications were made to the electrical system to reduce the risk of the battery overheating or catching fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-340"><a href="#cite_note-340">[339]</a></sup> This was the first time that the FAA had grounded an airliner type since 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcBat1_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbcBat1-341">[340]</a></sup> Industry experts disagreed on consequences of the grounding: Airbus was confident that Boeing would resolve the issue<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[341]</a></sup> and that no airlines will switch plane type,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-343"><a href="#cite_note-343">[342]</a></sup> while other experts saw the problem as "costly"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-344"><a href="#cite_note-344">[343]</a></sup> and "could take upwards of a year".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-345"><a href="#cite_note-345">[344]</a></sup></p>
<p>The FAA also conducted an extensive review of the 787's critical systems. The focus of the review was on the safety of the <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery#Safety" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion batteries</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcBat1_341-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbcBat1-341">[340]</a></sup> made of <a href="/wiki/Lithium_cobalt_oxide" title="Lithium cobalt oxide">lithium cobalt oxide</a> (LiCoO<sub>2</sub>). The 787 battery contract was signed in 2005,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gs2005_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-gs2005-198">[198]</a></sup> when this was the only type of lithium aerospace battery available, but since then newer and safer<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-346"><a href="#cite_note-346">[345]</a></sup> types (such as <a href="/wiki/Lithium_iron_phosphate_battery" title="Lithium iron phosphate battery">LiFePO</a>), which provide less reaction energy during <a href="/wiki/Thermal_runaway" title="Thermal runaway">thermal runaway</a>, have become available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tuCo_347-0"><a href="#cite_note-tuCo-347">[346]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-348"><a href="#cite_note-348">[347]</a></sup> FAA approved a 787 battery in 2007 with nine "special conditions".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-faa9_349-0"><a href="#cite_note-faa9-349">[348]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-350"><a href="#cite_note-350">[349]</a></sup> A battery approved by FAA (through Mobile Power Solutions) was made by Rose Electronics using Kokam cells;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supIv_351-0"><a href="#cite_note-supIv-351">[350]</a></sup> the batteries installed in the 787 are made by Yuasa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meggitt_195-1"><a href="#cite_note-meggitt-195">[195]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 20, the NTSB declared that overvoltage was not the cause of the Boston incident, as voltage did not exceed the battery limit of 32 V,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-352"><a href="#cite_note-352">[351]</a></sup> and the charging unit passed tests. The battery had signs of <a href="/wiki/Short_circuit" title="Short circuit">short circuiting</a> and thermal runaway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-353"><a href="#cite_note-353">[352]</a></sup> Despite this, by January 24, the NTSB had not yet pinpointed the cause of the Boston fire; the FAA would not allow U.S.-based 787s to fly again until the problem was found and corrected. In a press briefing that day, NTSB Chairwoman <a href="/wiki/Deborah_Hersman" title="Deborah Hersman">Deborah Hersman</a> said that the NTSB had found evidence of failure of multiple safety systems designed to prevent these battery problems, and stated that fire must never happen on an airplane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-354"><a href="#cite_note-354">[353]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Japan_Transport_Safety_Board" title="Japan Transport Safety Board">Japan Transport Safety Board</a> (JTSB) has said on January 23 that the battery in ANA jets in Japan reached a maximum voltage of 31 V (below the 32 V limit like the Boston JAL 787), but had a sudden unexplained voltage drop<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mitra_355-0"><a href="#cite_note-mitra-355">[354]</a></sup> to near zero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concern_356-0"><a href="#cite_note-concern-356">[355]</a></sup> All cells had signs of thermal damage prior to runaway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-357"><a href="#cite_note-357">[356]</a></sup> ANA and JAL had replaced several 787 batteries before the mishaps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-concern_356-1"><a href="#cite_note-concern-356">[355]</a></sup> As of January 29, 2013, JTSB approved the Yuasa factory <a href="/wiki/Quality_control" title="Quality control">quality control</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-358"><a href="#cite_note-358">[357]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-359"><a href="#cite_note-359">[358]</a></sup> while the NTSB examined the Boston battery for defects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-360"><a href="#cite_note-360">[359]</a></sup> The failure rate, with two major battery thermal runaway events in 100,000 flight hours, was much higher than the rate of one in 10 million flight hours predicted by Boeing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-avherald1_336-1"><a href="#cite_note-avherald1-336">[335]</a></sup></p>
<p>The only American airline that operated the Dreamliner at the time was United Airlines, which had six.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-361"><a href="#cite_note-361">[360]</a></sup> Chile's <a href="/wiki/Directorate_General_of_Civil_Aviation_(Chile)" title="Directorate General of Civil Aviation (Chile)">Directorate General of Civil Aviation</a> (DGAC) grounded LAN Airlines' three 787s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-362"><a href="#cite_note-362">[361]</a></sup> The Indian <a href="/wiki/Directorate_General_of_Civil_Aviation_(India)" title="Directorate General of Civil Aviation (India)">Directorate General of Civil Aviation</a> (DGCA) directed Air India to ground its six Dreamliners. The Japanese Transport Ministry made the ANA and JAL groundings official and indefinite following the FAA announcement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DGCA_India_363-0"><a href="#cite_note-DGCA_India-363">[362]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/European_Aviation_Safety_Agency" title="European Aviation Safety Agency">European Aviation Safety Agency</a> also followed the FAA's advice and grounded the only two European 787s operated by <a href="/wiki/LOT_Polish_Airlines" title="LOT Polish Airlines">LOT Polish Airlines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-364"><a href="#cite_note-364">[363]</a></sup> Qatar Airways grounded their five Dreamliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-365"><a href="#cite_note-365">[364]</a></sup> Ethiopian Airlines was the final operator to temporarily ground its four Dreamliners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_others_ground_366-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_others_ground-366">[365]</a></sup> By January 17, 2013, all 50 of the aircraft delivered to date had been grounded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reuters_others_ground_366-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reuters_others_ground-366">[365]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-367"><a href="#cite_note-367">[366]</a></sup> On January 18, Boeing halted 787 deliveries until the battery problem was resolved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-368"><a href="#cite_note-368">[367]</a></sup></p>
<p>On February 7, 2013, the FAA gave approval for Boeing to conduct 787 test flights to gather additional data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-369"><a href="#cite_note-369">[368]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-370"><a href="#cite_note-370">[369]</a></sup> In February 2013, FAA oversight of the 787's 2007 safety approval and certification was under scrutiny.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-371"><a href="#cite_note-371">[370]</a></sup> On March 7, 2013, the NTSB released an interim factual report about the Boston battery fire on January 7, 2013. The investigation<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-372"><a href="#cite_note-372">[371]</a></sup> stated that "heavy smoke and fire coming from the front of the APU battery case." Firefighters "tried fire extinguishing, but smoke and flame (flame size about 3 inches) did not stop".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-373"><a href="#cite_note-373">[372]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-374"><a href="#cite_note-374">[373]</a></sup></p>
<p>Boeing completed its final tests on a revised battery design on April 5, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-375"><a href="#cite_note-375">[374]</a></sup> The FAA approved Boeing's revised battery design with three additional, overlapping protection methods on April 19, 2013. The FAA published a directive on April 25 to provide instructions for retrofitting battery hardware before the 787s could return to flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-376"><a href="#cite_note-376">[375]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_787_returns_377-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_787_returns-377">[376]</a></sup> The repairs were expected to be completed in weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-378"><a href="#cite_note-378">[377]</a></sup> Following the FAA approval in the U.S.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gates_379-0"><a href="#cite_note-gates-379">[378]</a></sup> Japan gave permission for passenger airlines to resume Boeing 787 flights in the country effective April 26, 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-380"><a href="#cite_note-380">[379]</a></sup> On April 27, 2013, Ethiopian Airlines took a 787 on the model's first commercial flight after battery system modifications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_787_returns_377-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_787_returns-377">[376]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gates_379-1"><a href="#cite_note-gates-379">[378]</a></sup></p>
<p>On January 14, 2014, a battery in a JAL 787 emitted smoke from the battery's protection exhaust while the aircraft was undergoing pre-flight maintenance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-381"><a href="#cite_note-381">[380]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-382"><a href="#cite_note-382">[381]</a></sup> The battery partially melted in the incident;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-383"><a href="#cite_note-383">[382]</a></sup> one of its eight lithium-ion cells had its relief port vent and fluid sprayed inside the battery's container.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-384"><a href="#cite_note-384">[383]</a></sup> It was later reported that the battery may have reached a temperature as high as 1,220 °F (660 °C), and that Boeing did not understand the root cause of the failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-385"><a href="#cite_note-385">[384]</a></sup></p>
<p>The NTSB has criticized FAA, Boeing and the battery manufacturer for the faults;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-knud_386-0"><a href="#cite_note-knud-386">[385]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-387"><a href="#cite_note-387">[386]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-388"><a href="#cite_note-388">[387]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-389"><a href="#cite_note-389">[388]</a></sup> it also criticized the flight data recorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-390"><a href="#cite_note-390">[389]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aircraft_on_display">Aircraft on display</span></h2>

<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-787_O_D_summ-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-787_O_D_summ_1-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a class="external text" href="http://active.boeing.com/commercial/orders/displaystandardreport.cfm?cboCurrentModel=787&amp;optReportType=AllModels&amp;cboAllModel=787&amp;ViewReportF=View+Report" rel="nofollow">"Boeing 787: Orders and Deliveries (updated monthly)"</a>. <i>The Boeing Company</i>.
